theorem imo_1983_p6
  (a b c : ℝ)
  (h₀ : 0 < a ∧ 0 < b ∧ 0 < c)

  have h_main : 0 ≤ a^2 * b * (a - b) + b^2 * c * (b - c) + c^2 * a * (c - a) := by
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (b - c), sq_nonneg (c - a),

      mul_pos (sub_pos.mpr h₁) (sub_pos.mpr h₃),

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that:
\\[ 0 \\leq a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) + c^2 a (c - a). \\]

#### Step 1: Understand the Expression
The expression is a sum of three terms:
1. \\( a^2 b (a - b) = a^3 b - a^2 b^2 \\)
2. \\( b^2 c (b - c) = b^3 c - b^2 c^2 \\)
3. \\( c^2 a (c - a) = c^3 a - c^2 a^2 \\)

So, the entire expression is:
\\[ a^3 b - a^2 b^2 + b^3 c - b^2 c^2 + c^3 a - c^2 a^2. \\]

#### Step 2: Symmetry and Rearrangement
Notice that the expression is not symmetric, but we can rearrange it to group terms involving \\( a, b, c \\). 

Alternatively, we can think of the expression as:
\\[ a^3 b + b^3 c + c^3 a - (a^2 b^2 + b^2 c^2 + c^2 a^2). \\]

#### Step 3: Use the Triangle Inequality
Given the conditions \\( a, b, c > 0 \\) and \\( c < a + b \\), \\( b < a + c \\), \\( a < b + c \\), we can use the triangle inequalities to bound the terms. However, this is not directly helpful here.

But this doesn't seem immediately helpful. Alternatively, we can consider the expression modulo some symmetry.

#### Step 8: Use Known Inequalities

1. \\( a - b \\geq 0 \\)
2. \\( b - c \\geq 0 \\)

Thus, the terms \\( a^2 b (a - b) \\geq 0 \\), \\( b^2 c (b - c) \\geq 0 \\), and \\( c^2 a (c - a) \\leq 0 \\).

But the first two terms are non-negative, and the third is non-positive. The sum of the first two terms is:
\\[ a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) \\geq 0 \\]
because \\( a^2 b (a - b) \\geq 0 \\) and \\( b^2 c (b - c) \\geq 0 \\) (since \\( a \\geq b \\geq c > 0 \\), \\( a - b \\geq 0 \\), \\( b - c \\geq 0 \\), and \\( a, b, c > 0 \\)).

But the third term is:
\\[ c^2 a (c - a) \\leq 0 \\]
because \\( c - a \\leq 0 \\).

\\[ a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) + c^2 a (c - a) \\geq 0 \\]
because the first two terms are non-negative and the third is non-positive, but the first two terms dominate the third.

But we need to be careful. The assumption \\( a \\geq b \\geq c > 0 \\) is not necessarily true. 

#### Step 9: General Approach

\\[ a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) \\geq c^2 a (a - c) \\]
under the assumption \\( a \\geq b \\geq c \\). 

But this is not straightforward. 

#### Step 14: Use the SOS (Sum of Squares) Method
Alternatively, we can write the expression as a sum of squares. 

However, this seems complicated. 

#### Step 15: Use the AM-GM Inequality
Alternatively, we can use the **AM-GM Inequality** to bound the terms. 

But it's not immediately clear how to apply it here. 

#### Step 16: Use the **Titu's Lemma**
This is not directly helpful. 

#### Step 17: Use the **Rearrangement Inequality**
Assume \\( a \\geq b \\geq c > 0 \\). Then:
1. \\( a^2 b (a - b) \\geq 0 \\)
2. \\( b^2 c (b - c) \\geq 0 \\)
3. \\( c^2 a (c - a) \\leq 0 \\)

\\[ a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) + c^2 a (c - a) \\geq 0. \\]

This is because the first two terms are non-negative and the third is non-positive, and the first two terms dominate the third. 

#### Step 18: Verification
To verify, consider the case \\( a = b = c \\):
\\[ a^2 b (a - b) + b^2 c (b - c) + c^2 a (c - a) = 0. \\]

For other cases, the expression is non-negative. 

#### Step 19: Conclusion
The expression is always non-negative under the given conditions. 

### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan

1. **Assume an Ordering**: Without loss of generality, assume \\( a \\geq b \\geq c > 0 \\). This is because the expression is symmetric under cyclic permutations.
2. **Sign Analysis**:
   - \\( a^2 b (a - b) \\geq 0 \\) because \\( a \\geq b \\) and \\( a, b > 0 \\).

theorem algebra_sqineq_unitcircatbpabsamblt1
  (a b: ℝ)
  (h₀ : a^2 + b^2 = 1) :
  a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1 := by
  have h_main : a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1 := by
    cases' le_total a b with h h <;> simp_all [abs_of_nonneg, abs_of_nonpos, sub_nonneg, sub_nonpos]
    <;>
    (try
      nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (a + b), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b - 1 / 2),
        sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a - b - 1), sq_nonneg (a - b + 1),
        sq_nonneg (a + b - 1), sq_nonneg (a + b + 1)]) <;>
    (try
      {
        nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (a + b), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b - 1 / 2),
          sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a - b - 1), sq_nonneg (a - b + 1),
          sq_nonneg (a + b - 1), sq_nonneg (a + b + 1)]
      }) <;>
    (try
      {
        cases' le_total 0 (a - b) with h₁ h₁ <;>
        cases' le_total 0 (a + b) with h₂ h₂ <;>
        simp_all [abs_of_nonneg, abs_of_nonpos, sub_nonneg, sub_nonpos] <;>
        nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (a + b), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b - 1 / 2),
          sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a - b - 1), sq_nonneg (a - b + 1),
          sq_nonneg (a + b - 1), sq_nonneg (a + b + 1)]
      }) <;>
    (try
      {
        nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (a + b), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b - 1 / 2),
          sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a - b - 1), sq_nonneg (a - b + 1),
          sq_nonneg (a + b - 1), sq_nonneg (a + b + 1)]
      })
    <;>
    nlinarith [sq_nonneg (a - b), sq_nonneg (a + b), sq_nonneg (a - 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b - 1 / 2),
      sq_nonneg (a + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (b + 1 / 2), sq_nonneg (a - b - 1), sq_nonneg (a - b + 1),
      sq_nonneg (a + b - 1), sq_nonneg (a + b + 1)]
  exact h_main

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We need to prove that for real numbers `a` and `b` such that `a² + b² = 1`, the inequality `a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1` holds.

**Key Observations:**
1. The condition `a² + b² = 1` suggests that `(a, b)` lies on the unit circle in the plane.
2. The expression `a * b` is the dot product of `(a, b)` with itself, scaled by `1/2`, i.e., `(a² + b²)/2 = 1/2`. However, this is not directly relevant here.
3. The term `|a - b|` is the distance between `a` and `b` on the real line.
4. The maximum of `a * b` under `a² + b² = 1` is `1/2` (achieved when `a = b = ±√2/2`).
5. The maximum of `|a - b|` under `a² + b² = 1` is `√2` (achieved when `a = 1, b = 0` or `a = 0, b = 1`).
6. The product `a * b + |a - b|` is maximized when `a = b = ±√2/2` and `|a - b| = 0`, giving `(1/2) + 0 = 1/2 < 1`. However, this is not the maximum. The actual maximum is `1`, achieved when `a = 1, b = 0` or `a = 0, b = 1`, giving `0 + 1 = 1`.

But wait, let's check the case `a = 1, b = 0`:
- `a² + b² = 1 + 0 = 1` ✔️
- `a * b + |a - b| = 0 + 1 = 1` ✔️

Similarly, `a = 0, b = 1`:
- `a² + b² = 0 + 1 = 1` ✔️
- `a * b + |a - b| = 0 + 1 = 1` ✔️

This suggests that the maximum is `1`, and the inequality is correct.

But is this the maximum? Let's consider `a = b = 0`:
- `a² + b² = 0 + 0 = 0 ≠ 1` ✗ (invalid case).

Alternatively, consider `a = √2/2, b = √2/2`:
- `a² + b² = 1/2 + 1/2 = 1` ✔️
- `a * b + |a - b| = (1/2) + 0 = 1/2 < 1` ✔️

This is less than `1`.

Another case: `a = 1/√2, b = -1/√2`:
- `a² + b² = 1/2 + 1/2 = 1` ✔️
- `a * b + |a - b| = (1/2) + √2 ≈ 1.914 > 1` ✗ (but `√2 ≈ 1.414`, so `(1/2) + √2 ≈ 1.914 > 1`).

This is incorrect because `√2 ≈ 1.414`, so `(1/2) + √2 ≈ 1.914 > 1`, but `a * b + |a - b| = (1/2) + √2 ≈ 1.914 > 1` is not a valid case because `a² + b² = 1/2 + 1/2 = 1` is satisfied, but `a * b = -1/2` and `|a - b| = √2`, so `a * b + |a - b| = -1/2 + √2 ≈ 0.914 < 1`.

This suggests that the maximum is `1`, achieved when `a = 1, b = 0` or `a = 0, b = 1`.

**Proof Sketch:**
1. We can assume without loss of generality that `a ≥ b` (since `|a - b| = |b - a|`).
2. The condition `a² + b² = 1` implies that `a` and `b` are in `[-1, 1]`.
3. We can consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b`:
   - If `a ≥ b`, then `|a - b| = a - b`, and the inequality becomes `a * b + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `a * b + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - If `a ≤ b`, then `|a - b| = b - a`, and the inequality becomes `a * b + b - a ≤ 1`, i.e., `a * b + b - a - 1 ≤ 0`.
4. We can use the fact that `a² + b² = 1` to bound the terms.
5. The maximum of `a * b + a - b` under `a² + b² = 1` is `1` (achieved at `(1, 0)` and `(0, 1)`), and similarly for `a * b + b - a`.

But to avoid casework, we can use the following approach:
1. The expression `a * b + |a - b|` is maximized when `a` and `b` are as far apart as possible under the constraint `a² + b² = 1`.
2. The maximum of `|a - b|` under `a² + b² = 1` is `√2` (achieved at `(1, 0)` and `(0, 1)`), and `a * b` is minimized when `a` and `b` are as far apart as possible, i.e., `a * b = -1/2` (achieved at `(√2/2, -√2/2)`).
3. The maximum of `a * b + |a - b|` is `1` (achieved at `(1, 0)` and `(0, 1)`).

But to prove this directly, we can use the following:
1. We know that `(a - b)² ≥ 0`, so `a² + b² ≥ 2ab`, i.e., `1 ≥ 2ab` or `ab ≤ 1/2`.
2. We can also use the identity `(a + b)² ≥ 0` to get `a² + b² + 2ab ≥ 0`, i.e., `1 + 2ab ≥ 0` or `ab ≥ -1/2`.
3. The term `|a - b|` can be bounded using `(a - b)² ≤ 2(a² + b²) = 2`, i.e., `|a - b| ≤ √2`.
4. The product `a * b + |a - b|` is maximized when `a * b` is maximized and `|a - b|` is maximized, i.e., when `a = 1, b = 0` or `a = 0, b = 1`.

But to avoid casework, we can use the following:
1. The maximum of `a * b + |a - b|` under `a² + b² = 1` is `1`, achieved at `(1, 0)` and `(0, 1)`.
2. To prove this, we can use the fact that `a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1` is equivalent to `a * b ≤ 1 - |a - b|`.
3. We can consider the cases `a ≥ b` and `a ≤ b` separately.

**Case 1: `a ≥ b`**
- `|a - b| = a - b`, so the inequality becomes `a * b + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `a * b + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.
- We can write `a * b = ab` and `a² + b² = 1`, so `a * b ≤ (a² + b²)/2 = 1/2` (by AM-GM).
- The inequality becomes `ab + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.
- We can use `a² + b² = 1` to express `a` in terms of `b` or vice versa, but this seems complicated.
- Alternatively, we can use the fact that `a * b ≤ 1/2` and `a - b ≤ √2` (since `(a - b)² ≤ 2(a² + b²) = 2`).
- But this is not directly helpful.

**Case 2: `a ≤ b`**
- `|a - b| = b - a`, so the inequality becomes `a * b + b - a ≤ 1`, i.e., `a * b + b - a - 1 ≤ 0`.
- Similarly, `a * b ≤ 1/2` and `b - a ≤ √2`, but this is not directly helpful.

**Simpler Approach:**
We can use the identity `(a - b)² ≥ 0` to get `a² + b² ≥ 2ab`, i.e., `1 ≥ 2ab` or `ab ≤ 1/2`.
Similarly, `(a + b)² ≥ 0` gives `a² + b² + 2ab ≥ 0` or `1 + 2ab ≥ 0` or `ab ≥ -1/2`.

We can also use the identity `|a - b|² = a² + b² - 2ab = 1 - 2ab`, so `|a - b| = √(1 - 2ab)`.

Thus, the inequality becomes `ab + √(1 - 2ab) ≤ 1`.

This is a one-variable inequality in `x = ab`, where `x ∈ [-1/2, 1/2]` (since `ab ≤ 1/2` and `ab ≥ -1/2`).

The inequality becomes `x + √(1 - 2x) ≤ 1`.

We can check the maximum of `f(x) = x + √(1 - 2x)` for `x ∈ [-1/2, 1/2]`:
- The derivative is `f'(x) = 1 + (-2)/(2√(1 - 2x)) = 1 - 1/√(1 - 2x)`.
- The critical point is when `f'(x) = 0`, i.e., `√(1 - 2x) = 1` or `1 - 2x = 1` or `x = 0`.
- At `x = 0`, `f(0) = 0 + 1 = 1`.
- At the endpoints:
  - `x = 1/2`: `f(1/2) = 1/2 + √(1 - 1) = 1/2 + 0 = 1/2 < 1`.
  - `x = -1/2`: `f(-1/2) = -1/2 + √(1 - (-1)) = -1/2 + √2 ≈ -0.5 + 1.414 ≈ 0.914 < 1`.
Thus, the maximum is `1` at `x = 0` (i.e., `ab = 0`).

But we need to ensure that `ab ≤ 1/2` and `ab ≥ -1/2` are satisfied.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that `a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1` is equivalent to `a * b ≤ 1 - |a - b|`.

But this is not straightforward. 

**Better Approach:**
We can use the identity `(a - b)² ≥ 0` to get `a² + b² ≥ 2ab`, i.e., `1 ≥ 2ab` or `ab ≤ 1/2`.
Similarly, `(a + b)² ≥ 0` gives `a² + b² + 2ab ≥ 0` or `1 + 2ab ≥ 0` or `ab ≥ -1/2`.

Thus, `ab ∈ [-1/2, 1/2]`.

We can consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b`:
1. If `a ≥ b`, then `|a - b| = a - b`, and the inequality becomes `ab + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `ab + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - We can write `a = b + t` where `t ≥ 0`, and substitute into the condition `a² + b² = 1`:
     `(b + t)² + b² = 1` ⇒ `2b² + 2bt + t² = 1`.
   - The inequality becomes `(b + t)b + (b + t) - b - 1 ≤ 0` ⇒ `b² + bt + t - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - We can use the quadratic in `b` to find the maximum.
2. If `a ≤ b`, then `|a - b| = b - a`, and the inequality becomes `ab + b - a ≤ 1`, i.e., `ab + b - a - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - Similarly, we can write `b = a + t` where `t ≥ 0`, and substitute into the condition `a² + b² = 1`:
     `a² + (a + t)² = 1` ⇒ `2a² + 2at + t² = 1`.
   - The inequality becomes `a(a + t) + (a + t) - a - 1 ≤ 0` ⇒ `a² + at + t - 1 ≤ 0`.

But this seems complicated. 

**Simpler Solution:**
We can use the fact that `a² + b² = 1` to parameterize `a` and `b` in terms of an angle `θ`:
`a = cos θ`, `b = sin θ`.

Then the inequality becomes:
`cos θ * sin θ + |cos θ - sin θ| ≤ 1`.

We know that `cos θ * sin θ = (cos 2θ)/2`, and `|cos θ - sin θ| = |√2 * cos(θ + π/4)| = √2 |cos(θ + π/4)| ≤ √2`.

But this is not directly helpful. 

Alternatively, we can use the identity:
`(a - b)² ≥ 0` ⇒ `a² + b² ≥ 2ab` ⇒ `1 ≥ 2ab` ⇒ `ab ≤ 1/2`.
`(a + b)² ≥ 0` ⇒ `a² + b² + 2ab ≥ 0` ⇒ `1 + 2ab ≥ 0` ⇒ `ab ≥ -1/2`.

Thus, `ab ∈ [-1/2, 1/2]`.

We can consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b`:
1. If `a ≥ b`, then `|a - b| = a - b`, and the inequality becomes `ab + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `ab + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - We can write `a = b + t` where `t ≥ 0`, and substitute into the condition `a² + b² = 1`:
     `(b + t)² + b² = 1` ⇒ `2b² + 2bt + t² = 1`.
   - The inequality becomes `(b + t)b + (b + t) - b - 1 ≤ 0` ⇒ `b² + bt + t - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - We can use the quadratic in `b` to find the maximum.
2. If `a ≤ b`, then `|a - b| = b - a`, and the inequality becomes `ab + b - a ≤ 1`, i.e., `ab + b - a - 1 ≤ 0`.
   - Similarly, we can write `b = a + t` where `t ≥ 0`, and substitute into the condition `a² + b² = 1`:
     `a² + (a + t)² = 1` ⇒ `2a² + 2at + t² = 1`.
   - The inequality becomes `a(a + t) + (a + t) - a - 1 ≤ 0` ⇒ `a² + at + t - 1 ≤ 0`.

But this seems too involved. 

**Final Approach:**
We can use the fact that `a * b + |a - b| ≤ 1` is equivalent to `a * b ≤ 1 - |a - b|`.

But this is not straightforward. 

Instead, we can use the following identity:
`(a - b)² ≥ 0` ⇒ `a² + b² ≥ 2ab` ⇒ `1 ≥ 2ab` ⇒ `ab ≤ 1/2`.
`(a + b)² ≥ 0` ⇒ `a² + b² + 2ab ≥ 0` ⇒ `1 + 2ab ≥ 0` ⇒ `ab ≥ -1/2`.

Thus, `ab ∈ [-1/2, 1/2]`.

We can consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b`:
1. If `a ≥ b`, then `|a - b| = a - b`, and the inequality becomes `ab + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `ab + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.

   - The inequality becomes `a(a + t) + (a + t) - a - 1 ≤ 0` ⇒ `a² + at + t - 1 ≤ 0`.

But this seems too involved. 

**Conclusion:**
The most straightforward approach is to consider the cases based on the sign of `a - b` and use the fact that `a² + b² = 1` to bound the terms.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Case `a ≥ b`:**
   - `|a - b| = a - b`.
   - The inequality becomes `a * b + a - b ≤ 1`, i.e., `a * b + a - b - 1 ≤ 0`.

   - In both cases, use the bounds `a * b ≤ 1/2` and `a - b ≤ √2` (or similar) to prove the inequality.

name = "mathd_algebra_398"
pass = 2

verified = true

proof = """
theorem mathd_algebra_398
  (a b c : ℝ)
  (h₀ : 0 < a ∧ 0 < b ∧ 0 < c)
  (h₁ : 9 * b = 20 * c)
  (h₂ : 7 * a = 4 * b) :
  63 * a = 80 * c := by
  have h_b_in_terms_of_c : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by
    have h₃ : b = (20 / 9 : ℝ) * c := by
      -- Solve for b in terms of c using the given equation 9 * b = 20 * c
      apply Eq.symm
      -- Use linear arithmetic to solve for b
      nlinarith
    exact h₃
  
  have h_a_in_terms_of_c : a = (80 / 63 : ℝ) * c := by
    have h₃ : 7 * a = 4 * b := h₂
    rw [h_b_in_terms_of_c] at h₃
    -- Substitute b = (20 / 9) * c into the equation 7 * a = 4 * b
    ring_nf at h₃ ⊢
    -- Simplify the equation to solve for a in terms of c
    nlinarith
  
  have h_main : 63 * a = 80 * c := by
    have h₃ : a = (80 / 63 : ℝ) * c := h_a_in_terms_of_c
    rw [h₃]
    <;> ring_nf
    <;> nlinarith
  
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given three positive real numbers \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) with the following relationships:
1. \\(9b = 20c\\)
2. \\(7a = 4b\\)

We need to prove that \\(63a = 80c\\).

**Approach:**
1. From \\(9b = 20c\\), we can express \\(b\\) in terms of \\(c\\) as \\(b = \\frac{20}{9}c\\).
2. Substitute this expression for \\(b\\) into the second equation \\(7a = 4b\\) to find an expression for \\(a\\) in terms of \\(c\\).
3. Substitute the expression for \\(a\\) into the goal \\(63a = 80c\\) and verify that it holds.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. From \\(9b = 20c\\), we get \\(b = \\frac{20}{9}c\\).
2. Substitute \\(b = \\frac{20}{9}c\\) into \\(7a = 4b\\):
   \\[
   7a = 4 \\cdot \\frac{20}{9}c = \\frac{80}{9}c.
   \\]
   Multiply both sides by \\(\\frac{9}{7}\\) to get:
   \\[
   a = \\frac{80}{63}c.
   \\]
3. Now, multiply both sides of \\(a = \\frac{80}{63}c\\) by \\(63\\) to get:
   \\[
   63a = 80c,
   \\]
   which is the desired result.

Alternatively, we can avoid solving for \\(a\\) in terms of \\(c\\) by directly manipulating the given equations. Here's an alternative approach:

1. From \\(7a = 4b\\), we get \\(b = \\frac{7}{4}a\\).
2. Substitute \\(b = \\frac{7}{4}a\\) into \\(9b = 20c\\):
   \\[
   9 \\cdot \\frac{7}{4}a = 20c \\implies \\frac{63}{4}a = 20c.
   \\]
   Multiply both sides by \\(\\frac{4}{63}\\) to get:
   \\[
   a = \\frac{80}{63}c.
   \\]
   This is the same as above, so we arrive at the same conclusion.

**Verification:**
Multiply both sides of \\(a = \\frac{80}{63}c\\) by \\(63\\) to get \\(63a = 80c\\), which is the goal.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

theorem imo_1977_p6
  (f : ℕ → ℕ)
  (h₀ : ∀ n, 0 < f n)
  (h₁ : ∀ n, 0 < n → f (f n) < f (n + 1)) :
  ∀ n, 0 < n → f n = n := by
  have h_main : ∀ n, 0 < n → f n = n := by
    intro n hn
    have h₂ : ∀ n, 0 < n → f n = n := by
      intro n hn
      induction n using Nat.strong_induction_on with
      | h n ih =>
        match n with
        | 0 =>
          exfalso
          linarith
        | 1 =>
          have h₃ := h₁ 1 (by norm_num)
          have h₄ := h₀ 1
          have h₅ := h₀ 2
          have h₆ := h₀ 0
          simp at h₃ h₄ h₅ h₆ ⊢
          <;>
            (try omega) <;>
            (try
              {
                omega
              }) <;>
            (try
              {
                nlinarith
              }) <;>
            (try
              {
                linarith
              })
        | n + 2 =>
          have h₃ := h₁ (n + 1) (by linarith)
          have h₄ := ih (n + 1) (by linarith)
          have h₅ := ih (f (n + 1)) (by
            have h₅₁ := h₀ (n + 1)
            have h₅₂ : 0 < f (n + 1) := by linarith
            linarith)
          have h₆ := h₀ (n + 1)
          have h₇ := h₀ (f (n + 1))
          have h₈ := h₀ 0
          have h₉ := h₀ (n + 2)
          simp at h₃ h₄ h₅ h₆ h₇ h₈ h₉ ⊢
          <;>
            (try omega) <;>
            (try
              {
                cases n with
                | zero =>
                  simp_all [Nat.succ_eq_add_one, Nat.add_assoc]
                  <;>
                    (try omega) <;>
                    (try
                      {
                        nlinarith
                      }) <;>
                    (try
                      {
                        linarith
                      })
                | succ n =>
                  simp_all [Nat.succ_eq_add_one, Nat.add_assoc]

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's carefully understand the problem and the hypotheses:

1. **Hypotheses**:
   - For all `n ∈ ℕ`, `f(n) > 0` (i.e., `f(n) ≥ 1`).
   - For all `n ∈ ℕ` such that `n > 0`, `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)`.

2. **Goal**: Prove that for all `n ∈ ℕ` such that `n > 0`, `f(n) = n`.

#### Observations:
1. The function `f` is strictly increasing in a very specific way. The condition `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` is very strong.
2. The condition `f(n) > 0` is redundant for `n ≥ 1` because `f(n)` is a natural number and `f(n) > 0` is already implied by `f(n) ≥ 1` (but Lean's `Nat` is `0, 1, 2, ...`).
3. The condition `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` is only required for `n > 0`, but we can use it to derive properties of `f` for all `n > 0`.

#### Key Idea:
We will first show that `f(1) = 1` and then use induction to show that `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0`.

#### Step 1: Prove `f(1) = 1`
Assume for contradiction that `f(1) > 1`. Then, since `f(1) ≥ 1` (by `h₀`), we have `f(1) ≥ 2`.

But `f(f(1)) < f(2)` by `h₁` applied to `n = 1` (since `1 > 0`).

But `f(1) ≥ 2` implies `f(f(1)) ≥ f(2)`, because `f` is increasing (but we don't know that yet). This is a contradiction because `f(f(1)) < f(2)` and `f(f(1)) ≥ f(2)` would imply `f(2) < f(2)`, which is false.

But wait, we don't know if `f` is increasing. We need to be careful.

#### Step 2: Prove `f(n) ≥ n` for all `n > 0`
We will use strong induction.

**Base case (`n = 1`)**:
We have `f(1) > 0` (by `h₀`), but we need `f(1) ≥ 1`. This is already true because `f(1) ≥ 1` is given by `h₀`.

**Inductive step (`n > 1`)**:
Assume that for all `k < n`, `f(k) = k` (strong induction hypothesis). We need to show `f(n) = n`.

But we don't have a direct way to use the hypothesis `h₁` here. Instead, we can use the fact that `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` and the properties of `f`.

But we need to find a contradiction if `f(n) > n` or `f(n) < n`.

#### Step 3: Prove `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0`

We will prove that `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0` by strong induction.

**Base case (`n = 1`)**:
We need to show `f(1) = 1`.

Assume for contradiction that `f(1) > 1`. Then, since `f(1) ≥ 1` (by `h₀`), we have `f(1) ≥ 2`.

But `f(f(1)) < f(2)` by `h₁` applied to `n = 1` (since `1 > 0`).

But `f(1) ≥ 2` implies `f(f(1)) ≥ f(2)`, because `f` is increasing (but we don't know that yet). This is a contradiction because `f(f(1)) < f(2)` and `f(f(1)) ≥ f(2)` would imply `f(2) < f(2)`, which is false.

But we don't know if `f` is increasing. We need to be careful.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that `f(n) ≥ n` for all `n > 0` and `f(n) ≤ n` for all `n > 0` to get `f(n) = n`.

But we can directly prove `f(n) = n` by strong induction.

**Inductive step (`n > 1`)**:
Assume that for all `k < n`, `f(k) = k`. We need to show `f(n) = n`.

But we can use the condition `h₁` to derive a contradiction if `f(n) ≠ n`.

Suppose `f(n) > n`. Then, since `f(n) ≥ 1`, we have `f(n) ≥ n + 1`.

But `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)`.

But `f(n) ≥ n + 1` implies `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` (because `f` is increasing). This is a contradiction because `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` and `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` would imply `f(n + 1) < f(n + 1)`, which is false.

Similarly, if `f(n) < n`, then `f(n) ≤ n - 1` (since `f(n) ≥ 1`).

But `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)`.

But `f(n) ≤ n - 1` implies `f(f(n)) ≤ f(n - 1)`.

But we don't know if `f(n - 1) < f(n + 1)`, so this is not directly helpful.

But we can use the fact that `f(n) ≥ n` and `f(n) ≤ n` to get `f(n) = n`.

#### Step 4: Prove `f(n) ≥ n` for all `n > 0`

We will use strong induction.

**Base case (`n = 1`)**:
`f(1) ≥ 1` is true by `h₀`.

**Inductive step (`n > 1`)**:
Assume that for all `k < n`, `f(k) ≥ k`. We need to show `f(n) ≥ n`.

But we can use the condition `h₁` to derive a contradiction if `f(n) < n`.

Suppose `f(n) < n`. Then, since `f(n) ≥ 1`, we have `f(n) ≤ n - 1`.

But `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)`.

But `f(n) ≤ n - 1` implies `f(f(n)) ≤ f(n - 1)`.

But we don't know if `f(n - 1) < f(n + 1)`, so this is not directly helpful.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that `f(n) ≥ n` is true for all `n > 0` by contradiction.

#### Step 5: Prove `f(n) ≤ n` for all `n > 0`

We will use strong induction.

**Base case (`n = 1`)**:
`f(1) ≤ 1` is true by `h₁` applied to `n = 1` (but we need to check).

But `h₁` gives `f(f(1)) < f(2)`.

But we don't know `f(1)`, so this is not directly helpful.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that `f(n) ≥ n` and `f(n) ≤ n` to get `f(n) = n`.

But we can prove `f(n) ≤ n` by contradiction.

Assume `f(n) > n`. Then, since `f(n) ≥ 1`, we have `f(n) ≥ n + 1`.

But `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)`.

But `f(n) ≥ n + 1` implies `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` (because `f` is increasing). This is a contradiction because `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` and `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` would imply `f(n + 1) < f(n + 1)`, which is false.

Thus, `f(n) ≤ n` for all `n > 0`.

#### Step 6: Prove `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0`

From `f(n) ≥ n` and `f(n) ≤ n`, we get `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0`.

### Step 7: Abstract Plan

1. **Prove `f(n) ≥ n` for all `n > 0`**:
   - Use strong induction.
   - For the inductive step, assume `f(k) ≥ k` for all `k < n` and `n > 0`.
   - If `f(n) < n`, then `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` (by monotonicity of `f`), but `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` by `h₁`, leading to a contradiction.
   - Hence, `f(n) ≥ n`.

2. **Prove `f(n) ≤ n` for all `n > 0`**:
   - Use strong induction.
   - For the inductive step, assume `f(k) ≤ k` for all `k < n` and `n > 0`.
   - If `f(n) > n`, then `f(f(n)) ≥ f(n + 1)` (by monotonicity of `f`), but `f(f(n)) < f(n + 1)` by `h₁`, leading to a contradiction.
   - Hence, `f(n) ≤ n`.

3. **Conclude `f(n) = n` for all `n > 0`**:
   - Combine `f(n) ≥ n` and `f(n) ≤ n` to get `f(n) = n`.

    The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's carefully restate the problem and understand what we are given and what we need to prove.

**Given:**
1. `k` is a positive natural number (`0 < k`).
2. For all real numbers `x`, `y(x) = ∑ i ∈ Finset.range k, (cos(a_i + x) / 2^i)`.
3. `y(m) = 0` and `y(n) = 0`.

**To Prove:** There exists an integer `t` such that `m - n = t * π`.

**Observations:**
1. The function `y(x)` is a sum of terms involving `cos(a_i + x) / 2^i` for `i` from `0` to `k-1`.
2. The condition `y(m) = 0` and `y(n) = 0` means that for each `m` and `n`, the sum of the terms `cos(a_i + m) / 2^i` is `0`, and similarly for `n`.
3. The goal is to show that `m - n` is a multiple of `π`, i.e., `m - n = t * π` for some integer `t`.

**Key Idea:**
The cosine function `cos(θ)` is periodic with period `2π`, and it is even (`cos(-θ) = cos(θ)`). The sum `y(x)` is a sum of terms of the form `cos(a_i + x) / 2^i`. The condition `y(m) = 0` and `y(n) = 0` suggests that the sum of the terms `cos(a_i + m) / 2^i` is `0` and similarly for `n`.

However, the problem is simpler than it seems because the `a_i`'s are arbitrary real numbers, and the `2^i` terms are positive. The only way the sum of `cos(a_i + x) / 2^i` can be `0` is if each term `cos(a_i + x) / 2^i` is `0` individually. But this is not necessarily true because the `a_i`'s are arbitrary. 

But wait, the `a_i`'s are arbitrary real numbers, and the `2^i` terms are positive. The sum of `cos(a_i + x) / 2^i` is `0` for `x = m` and `x = n`. 

But we can consider the difference `y(m) - y(n) = 0 - 0 = 0`, i.e., `∑ ... (cos(a_i + m) - cos(a_i + n)) / 2^i = 0`. 

But `cos(a_i + m) - cos(a_i + n) = -2 * sin((a_i + m + a_i + n)/2) * sin((a_i + m - a_i - n)/2) = -2 * sin(a_i + (m + n)/2) * sin((m - n)/2)`. 

Thus, the sum becomes:
`∑_{i=0}^{k-1} (-2 * sin(a_i + (m + n)/2) * sin((m - n)/2)) / 2^i = 0`.

This can be rewritten as:
`-2 * sin((m - n)/2) * ∑_{i=0}^{k-1} sin(a_i + (m + n)/2) / 2^i = 0`.

Since `sin((m - n)/2) ≠ 0` is possible (unless `m = n`, in which case `m - n = 0 = 0 * π` is trivially true), we can divide both sides by `-2 * sin((m - n)/2) ≠ 0` to get:
`∑_{i=0}^{k-1} sin(a_i + (m + n)/2) / 2^i = 0`.

But this is not necessarily true unless `sin((m - n)/2) = 0`, i.e., `(m - n)/2 = π * t` for some integer `t`, i.e., `m - n = 2 * π * t`. 

But we need to prove that `m - n = t * π` for some integer `t`. 

This suggests that our initial approach is not directly leading to the desired conclusion.

Subtracting these two equations gives:
`m - n = π * (t_1 - t_2) = t * π` where `t = t_1 - t_2` is an integer.

#### Step 6: Use the Exact Value of \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right)\\)
We can use the exact value of \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right)\\). However, this is not straightforward. Instead, we can use the following identity:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

This can be verified numerically, but a rigorous proof is non-trivial.

#### Step 7: Use Complex Numbers and Roots of Unity
Consider the 14th roots of unity. The sum of the real parts of the roots \\(\\omega^k\\) for \\(k = 1, 2, \\dots, 13\\) is zero, where \\(\\omega = e^{i \\pi / 7}\\). However, this is not directly helpful.

#### Step 8: Use Symmetry and Known Results
A known result is that:
\\[ \\sum_{k=1}^6 \\cos\\left(\\frac{k\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\frac{1}{2}. \\]
This can be derived by considering the sum of the real parts of the roots of unity.

But our sum is:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

Notice that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\pi - \\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]
But \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right)\\) is not true. Wait, no:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\pi - \\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]
Thus:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

But this doesn't directly help. 

#### Step 9: Use a Different Approach
Instead, we can use the identity:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}. \\]

This can be verified by multiplying both sides by \\(2 \\sin(\\pi/7)\\) and using the product-to-sum identity repeatedly.

#### Step 10: Final Calculation
Multiply \\(S\\) by \\(2 \\sin(\\pi/7)\\):
\\[ 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) S = 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) \\cos(\\pi/7) - 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) \\cos(2\\pi/7) + 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) \\cos(3\\pi/7). \\]

Using the identity \\(2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\phi = \\sin(2\\theta) - \\sin(2\\phi)\\), we get:
\\[ 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) S = \\sin(2\\pi/7) - \\sin(\\pi/7) - \\sin(4\\pi/7) + \\sin(3\\pi/7) + \\sin(6\\pi/7) - \\sin(3\\pi/7). \\]

Simplify:
\\[ 2 \\sin(\\pi/7) S = \\sin(2\\pi/7) - \\sin(\\pi/7) - \\sin(4\\pi/7) + \\sin(6\\pi/7). \\]

But \\(\\sin(6\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi - \\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7)\\), and \\(\\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7)\\). Wait, no:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is incorrect. 

Actually:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is false. 

But:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is false. 

Instead, we can use the exact value:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is false. 

But:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is false. 

But:
\\[ \\sin(4\\pi/7) = \\sin(3\\pi/7) = \\sin(\\pi/7) \\] is false. 

This is getting complicated. 

#### Step 11: Use a Different Strategy

But our sum is:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{7}\\right) - \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

Notice that:
\\[ \\cos\\left(\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\left(\\pi - \\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right). \\]

### Detailed Proof and Analysis

**Problem Analysis:**
We are given two natural numbers `x` and `y` with the following properties:
1. `x ≡ 2 mod 3` (i.e., `x % 3 = 2`).
2. `y ≡ 4 mod 5` (i.e., `y % 5 = 4`).
3. `x ≡ y mod 10` (i.e., `x % 10 = y % 10`).

We need to prove that `14 ≤ x`.

**Approach:**
1. Understand the possible values of `x` modulo 3 and modulo 10.
   - From `x ≡ 2 mod 3`, `x` can be written as `x = 3k + 2` for some integer `k ≥ 0`.
   - From `x ≡ y mod 10`, we have `x ≡ y mod 10`.
2. Understand the possible values of `y` modulo 5 and modulo 10.
   - From `y ≡ 4 mod 5`, `y` can be written as `y = 5m + 4` for some integer `m ≥ 0`.
   - We can then find `x` modulo 10 in terms of `m` using `x ≡ y mod 10`.
3. Find a lower bound for `x` by considering the smallest possible `x` that satisfies all the given conditions.

**Detailed Steps:**
1. From `x ≡ 2 mod 3`, we have `x = 3k + 2` for some `k ≥ 0`.
2. From `x ≡ y mod 10`, we have `x ≡ y mod 10`. Substitute `y = 5m + 4` to get `x ≡ 5m + 4 mod 10`.
   - This means `x % 10 = (5m + 4) % 10`.
3. We can find all possible values of `x % 10` by considering all possible values of `5m + 4 % 10` for `m ≥ 0`:
   - `5m + 4 ≡ 4 mod 5` is always true, but we can compute `5m + 4 % 10` for `m ≥ 0`:
     - `m = 0`: `5*0 + 4 = 4` → `4 % 10 = 4`
     - `m = 1`: `5*1 + 4 = 9` → `9 % 10 = 9`
     - `m = 2`: `5*2 + 4 = 14` → `14 % 10 = 4`
     - `m = 3`: `5*3 + 4 = 19` → `19 % 10 = 9`
     - `m = 4`: `5*4 + 4 = 24` → `24 % 10 = 4`
     - `m = 5`: `5*5 + 4 = 29` → `29 % 10 = 9`
     - `m = 6`: `5*6 + 4 = 34` → `34 % 10 = 4`
     - `m = 7`: `5*7 + 4 = 39` → `39 % 10 = 9`
     - `m = 8`: `5*8 + 4 = 44` → `44 % 10 = 4`
     - `m = 9`: `5*9 + 4 = 49` → `49 % 10 = 9`
     - `m = 10`: `5*10 + 4 = 54` → `54 % 10 = 4`
     - And so on. The pattern is `4, 9, 4, 9, ...` for `m ≥ 0`.
   - Thus, `x % 10` can be `4` or `9`.
4. From `x ≡ 2 mod 3`, we can find the possible values of `x` modulo 30:
   - The general form is `x = 3k + 2` for `k ≥ 0`.
   - The possible values of `x` modulo 30 are `2, 5, 8, 11, 14, 17, 20, 23, 26, 29, ...` (i.e., `x ≡ 2, 5, 8, 11, 14, 17, 20, 23, 26, 29 mod 30`).
5. We need to find the smallest `x` such that `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 4 or 9 mod 10` (since `x % 10` is either `4` or `9`).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 4 mod 10` is `x = 14` (since `14 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `14 ≡ 4 mod 10`).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` is `x = 11` (since `11 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `11 ≡ 9 mod 10`).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 4 or 9 mod 10` is `x = 11` (if `x ≡ 9 mod 10`) or `x = 14` (if `x ≡ 4 mod 10`).
   - But we need to ensure that `x ≡ 4 or 9 mod 10` is correct. We can check:
     - `x = 11`: `11 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `11 ≡ 9 mod 10` → `x % 10 = 9`
     - `x = 14`: `14 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `14 ≡ 4 mod 10` → `x % 10 = 4`
     - `x = 17`: `17 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `17 ≡ 7 mod 10` → `x % 10 = 7` (but `7` is not `4` or `9`).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying all conditions is `x = 11` (`x ≡ 9 mod 10`).
   - But wait, `x = 11` is not a solution because `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` is not possible. Let's check:
     - `x = 11`: `11 % 3 = 2` and `11 % 10 = 1` ≠ `9`.
     - `x = 14`: `14 % 3 = 2` and `14 % 10 = 4` ≠ `9`.
     - `x = 17`: `17 % 3 = 2` and `17 % 10 = 7` ≠ `9`.
     - `x = 20`: `20 % 3 = 2` and `20 % 10 = 0` ≠ `9`.

   - But `x = 11` is invalid because `11 % 10 = 1 ≠ 9`.
   - Hmm, I made a mistake in the earlier analysis. The correct approach is to find all `x` such that `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ y mod 10` where `y ≡ 4 mod 5`.
   - Since `x ≡ y mod 10` and `y ≡ 4 mod 5`, we have `x ≡ 4 mod 10` or `x ≡ 9 mod 10` (as `y` can be `4` or `9` modulo 10).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 4 mod 10` is `x = 14` (`14 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `14 ≡ 4 mod 10`).
   - The smallest `x` satisfying `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` is `x = 29` (`29 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `29 ≡ 9 mod 10`).

   - Therefore, the smallest `x` is `14`, and all other `x` satisfying the conditions are `14 + 30k` for `k ≥ 0`.
   - Hence, `x ≥ 14` is the smallest possible value.

But wait, we can also have `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` is not possible because `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` would imply `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10`. But `9 ≡ 9 mod 10` is correct, but `2 ≡ 9 mod 3` is false (`2 ≡ 2 mod 3` and `9 ≡ 0 mod 3`). So `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 9 mod 10` is not possible.

But `x ≡ 2 mod 3` and `x ≡ 4 mod 10` is possible (`x = 14`).

Thus, the smallest `x` is `14`, and all other `x` are `≥ 14`.

But we need to ensure that no `x < 14` satisfies the conditions. Let's check:
- `x = 0`: `0 % 3 = 0 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 1`: `1 % 3 = 1 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 2`: `2 % 3 = 2` → `x % 3 = 2` is satisfied.
   - `x % 10 = 2` and `y % 5 = 4` → `y % 10` could be `4` or `9` (since `y = 5m + 4` → `y % 10` is `4` or `9`).
   - But `x % 10 = 2` and `y % 10` is `4` or `9` → `2 ≠ 4` and `2 ≠ 9` → `x % 10 ≠ y % 10` → `x ≢ y mod 10` → contradiction.
- `x = 3`: `3 % 3 = 0 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 4`: `4 % 3 = 1 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 5`: `5 % 3 = 2` → `x % 3 = 2` is satisfied.
   - `x % 10 = 5` and `y % 10` is `4` or `9` → `5 ≠ 4` and `5 ≠ 9` → `x % 10 ≠ y % 10` → `x ≢ y mod 10` → contradiction.
- `x = 6`: `6 % 3 = 0 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 7`: `7 % 3 = 1 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 8`: `8 % 3 = 2` → `x % 3 = 2` is satisfied.
   - `x % 10 = 8` and `y % 10` is `4` or `9` → `8 ≠ 4` and `8 ≠ 9` → `x % 10 ≠ y % 10` → `x ≢ y mod 10` → contradiction.
- `x = 9`: `9 % 3 = 0 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 10`: `10 % 3 = 1 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 11`: `11 % 3 = 2` → `x % 3 = 2` is satisfied.
   - `x % 10 = 1` and `y % 10` is `4` or `9` → `1 ≠ 4` and `1 ≠ 9` → `x % 10 ≠ y % 10` → `x ≢ y mod 10` → contradiction.
- `x = 12`: `12 % 3 = 0 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 13`: `13 % 3 = 1 ≠ 2`.
- `x = 14`: `14 % 3 = 2` → `x % 3 = 2` is satisfied.
   - `x % 10 = 4` and `y % 10` is `4` or `9` → `4 = 4` → `x % 10 = y % 10` → `x ≡ y mod 10` is satisfied.
   - Thus, `x = 14` is a valid solution.

Therefore, the smallest `x` satisfying all conditions is `14`, and all other `x` satisfying the conditions are `≥ 14`.

**Conclusion:**
The smallest `x` satisfying all the given conditions is `14`, and hence `14 ≤ x` is true.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Understand the conditions:**
   - `x ≡ 2 mod 3`.

theorem induction_nfactltnexpnm1ngt3
  (n : ℕ)
  (h₀ : 3 ≤ n) :
  n.factorial < n^(n - 1) := by
  have h_main : n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
    have h₁ : n ≥ 3 := by linarith
    have h₂ : n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
      have h₃ : ∀ n : ℕ, 3 ≤ n → n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
        intro n hn
        induction' hn with n hn IH
        · -- Base case: n = 3
          norm_num [Nat.factorial]
        · -- Inductive step: assume the statement holds for n, prove for n + 1
          cases n with
          | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be zero since hn : 3 ≤ n
          | succ n =>
            cases n with
            | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be one since hn : 3 ≤ n
            | succ n =>
              cases n with
              | zero => contradiction -- n cannot be two since hn : 3 ≤ n
              | succ n =>
                simp_all [Nat.factorial_succ, Nat.pow_succ, Nat.mul_comm, Nat.mul_assoc, Nat.mul_left_comm]
                -- Use nlinarith to handle the arithmetic and inequalities
                <;>
                (try omega) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    norm_num at *
                    <;>
                    ring_nf at *
                    <;>
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 2)]
                  }) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    simp_all [Nat.mul_comm, Nat.mul_assoc, Nat.mul_left_comm]
                    <;>
                    ring_nf at *
                    <;>
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 2)]
                  }) <;>
                (try
                  {
                    nlinarith [pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) n, pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 1),
                      pow_pos (by norm_num : (0 : ℕ) < 2) (n + 2)]
                  })
      exact h₃ n h₁
    exact h₂
  exact h_main

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to prove that for all integers `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^(n - 1)`.

#### Key Observations:
1. The factorial `n!` is the product of all positive integers up to `n`, while `n^(n - 1)` is `n` raised to the power of `n - 1`.
2. For `n ≥ 3`, `n!` grows much faster than `n^(n - 1)`, but we need a precise comparison.
3. The inequality `n! < n^(n - 1)` can be rewritten as `n! < n^n / n` for `n ≥ 3` (since `n - 1 = n - 1` is not directly helpful). However, this is not directly useful. Instead, we can use induction or direct comparison.

#### Direct Comparison Approach:
For `n ≥ 3`, we can compare `n!` and `n^(n - 1)` by considering the growth rates. 

1. For `n = 3`:
   - `3! = 6`
   - `3^(3 - 1) = 3^2 = 9`
   - `6 < 9` holds.
2. For `n = 4`:
   - `4! = 24`
   - `4^(4 - 1) = 4^3 = 64`
   - `24 < 64` holds.
3. For `n ≥ 5`, we can use induction or a more general inequality.

#### General Proof:
We can prove the inequality by induction on `n ≥ 3`.

**Base Case (`n = 3`):**
   - `3! = 6`
   - `3^(3 - 1) = 3^2 = 9`
   - `6 < 9` holds.

**Inductive Step (`n ≥ 4`):**
   Assume that `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`. We need to show that `(k + 1)! < (k + 1)^k`.

   - `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)` (by the inductive hypothesis).
   - We need to show that `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k`, i.e., `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.
   - Since `k ≥ 3`, `k + 1 > k` and `k - 1 ≥ 2`, so `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`.
   - Thus, `(k + 1)! < (k + 1) * (k + 1)^(k - 1) = (k + 1)^k`.

This completes the induction.

#### Alternative Direct Proof:
Alternatively, we can use the fact that for `n ≥ 3`, `n! < n^n` and `n^n ≤ n^(n - 1)` when `n ≥ 2` (since `n^n = n * n^(n - 1)` and `n ≥ 2` implies `n * n^(n - 1) ≥ n^(n - 1)`). However, this is not directly helpful because `n^(n - 1)` is not a factorial. 

But the inductive approach is straightforward and correct.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Base Case (`n = 3`):**
   - Compute `3! = 6` and `3^(3 - 1) = 9`.
   - Check `6 < 9`.

2. **Inductive Step (`n ≥ 4`):**
   - Assume `k! < k^(k - 1)` for some `k ≥ 3`.
   - Prove `(k + 1)! < (k + 1)^k`:
     - `(k + 1)! = (k + 1) * k! < (k + 1) * k^(k - 1)`.
     - Show `(k + 1) * k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^k` by proving `k^(k - 1) < (k + 1)^(k - 1)`, which is true because `k + 1 > k` and `k - 1 ≥ 2` for `k ≥ 3`.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem induction_nfactltnexpnm1ngt3
  (n : ℕ)
  (h₀ : 3 ≤ n) :
  n.factorial < n^(n - 1) := by
  have h_main : n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1) := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

### Explanation:
- The `have` statement `h_main` directly corresponds to the goal `n.factorial < n ^ (n - 1)`. The proof of `h_main` is marked as `sorry` and will be filled in later. The rest of the proof is trivial since `h_main` is exactly the goal.

This means we need to show that the biconditional `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is not true for all integers `a` and `b`. 

To do this, we will:
1. Find a counterexample where `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j)` is true, but `(∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is false.
2. Similarly, find a counterexample where `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j)` is false, but `(∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is true.

#### Case 10: `a = 5`, `b = 1`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false because `5` is not divisible by `2` and `1` is not divisible by `2`.
- `a² + b² = 25 + 1 = 26 = 8*3 + 2`, so `a² + b² = 8*k` is false for all `k` (since `26` is not divisible by `8`).
Thus, the biconditional is false because the left side is false and the right side is false.

#### Case 11: `a = 5`, `b = 3`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false because `5` is not divisible by `2` and `3` is not divisible by `2`.
- `a² + b² = 25 + 9 = 34 = 8*4 + 2`, so `a² + b² = 8*k` is false for all `k` (since `34` is not divisible by `8`).
Thus, the biconditional is false because the left side is false and the right side is false.

#### Case 12: `a = 6`, `b = 0`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is true with `i = 3`, `j = 0`.
- `a² + b² = 36 + 0 = 36 = 8*4 + 4`, so `a² + b² = 8*k` is false for all `k` (since `36` is not divisible by `8`).
Thus, the biconditional is false because the left side is true and the right side is false.

#### Case 13: `a = 6`, `b = 2`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is true with `i = 3`, `j = 1`.
- `a² + b² = 36 + 4 = 40 = 8*5`, so `∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k` is true with `k = 5`.
Thus, the biconditional is true.

#### Case 14: `a = 6`, `b = 4`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is true with `i = 3`, `j = 2`.
- `a² + b² = 36 + 16 = 52 = 8*6 + 4`, so `a² + b² = 8*k` is false for all `k` (since `52` is not divisible by `8`).
Thus, the biconditional is false because the left side is true and the right side is false.

#### Case 15: `a = 6`, `b = 6`
- `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is true with `i = 3`, `j = 3`.
- `a² + b² = 36 + 36 = 72 = 8*9`, so `∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k` is true with `k = 9`.
Thus, the biconditional is true.

#### General Observations
From the above cases, we can see that the biconditional is not true in general. The left side `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j)` is true when `a` and `b` are even, and false when `a` or `b` is odd. The right side `(∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is true when `a² + b² ≡ 0 mod 8`, i.e., when `a² + b²` is divisible by `8`. 

However, we can find cases where:
1. The left side is true and the right side is false:
   - `a = 1`, `b = 1` (`1 + 1 = 2` is not divisible by `8`).
2. The left side is false and the right side is true:
   - `a = 1`, `b = 3` (`1 + 9 = 10` is not divisible by `8`).
3. The left side is true and the right side is true:
   - `a = 2`, `b = 2` (`4 + 4 = 8` is divisible by `8`).
4. The left side is false and the right side is false:
   - `a = 3`, `b = 1` (`9 + 1 = 10` is not divisible by `8`).

Thus, the biconditional is false in general.

### Step 1: Abstract Plan

1. **Understand the Biconditional**:
   - The biconditional is `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)`.
   - We need to disprove that this is true for all `a`, `b`.

2. **Find Counterexamples**:
   - Find `a`, `b` such that:
     - `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j)` is true, and `(∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is false.
     - `(∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j)` is false, and `(∃ k, a² + b² = 8*k)` is true.

3. **Construct Counterexamples**:
   - `a = 1`, `b = 1`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 2` is not divisible by `8`.
   - `a = 1`, `b = 3`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 10` is not divisible by `8`.
   - `a = 3`, `b = 1`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 10` is not divisible by `8`.
   - `a = 1`, `b = 5`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 26` is not divisible by `8`.
   - `a = 1`, `b = 7`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 50` is not divisible by `8`.
   - `a = 1`, `b = 9`:
     - `∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b = 2*j` is false.
     - `a² + b² = 82` is not divisible by `8`.

4. **General Strategy**:
   - The left side is true when `a` and `b` are even, and false when `a` or `b` is odd.
   - The right side is true when `a² + b² ≡ 0 mod 8`, i.e., when `a` and `b` are even.
   - The biconditional is false when:
     - The left side is true and the right side is false.
     - The left side is false and the right side is true.

5. **Proof Sketch**:
   - Assume the biconditional is true for all `a`, `b`.
   - Find a contradiction by checking specific cases.

### Step 2: Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem numbertheory_notequiv2i2jasqbsqdiv8
  (a b : ℤ) :
  ¬ ((∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a^2 + b^2 = 8*k)) := by
  have h_main : ¬ ((∃ i j, a = 2*i ∧ b=2*j) ↔ (∃ k, a^2 + b^2 = 8*k)) := by
    sorry
  sorry
```

proof = """
theorem amc12a_2020_p10
  (n : ℕ)
  (h₀ : 0 < n)
  (h₁ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)) :
  (Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
  have h_main : n = 256 := by
    have h₂ : 0 < (n : ℝ) := by exact_mod_cast h₀
    have h₃ : Real.logb 16 n = Real.logb 2 n / 4 := by
      have h₄ : Real.logb 16 n = Real.log n / Real.log 16 := by
        rw [Real.logb]
        <;> simp [Real.logb]
        <;> ring_nf
      rw [h₄]
      have h₅ : Real.log 16 = Real.log (2 ^ 4) := by norm_num
      rw [h₅]
      have h₆ : Real.log (2 ^ 4) = 4 * Real.log 2 := by
        rw [Real.log_pow] <;> ring_nf <;> norm_num
      rw [h₆]
      have h₇ : Real.logb 2 n = Real.log n / Real.log 2 := by
        rw [Real.logb]
        <;> simp [Real.logb]

outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, recall that the logarithm base `b` of `x` is defined as `logb b x = log x / log b`. 

#### Step 1: Simplify the given equation
The given equation is:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) \\]

We can rewrite all logarithms in terms of base 2:
1. \\(\\log_{16} n = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{\\log_2 16} = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4}\\) because \\(16 = 2^4\\).
2. \\(\\log_4 n = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{\\log_2 4} = \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2}\\).
3. \\(\\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\log_4 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right)\\).
4. \\(\\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\right)\\).

Thus, the equation becomes:
\\[ \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\right) = \\log_4 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right) \\]

#### Step 2: Simplify the right-hand side
Recall that \\(\\log_4 x = \\frac{\\log_2 x}{2}\\). So:
\\[ \\log_4 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right) = \\frac{\\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right)}{2} \\]

#### Step 3: Simplify the left-hand side
\\[ \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{4} \\right) = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 \\]
because \\(\\log_2 4 = 2\\).

#### Step 4: Substitute back into the equation
The equation is now:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 2 = \\frac{\\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right)}{2} \\]

Multiply both sides by 2:
\\[ 2 \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right) \\]

#### Step 5: Further simplify the right-hand side
\\[ \\log_2 \\left( \\frac{\\log_2 n}{2} \\right) = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - \\log_2 2 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1 \\]

Thus, the equation becomes:
\\[ 2 \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 1 \\]

#### Step 6: Solve for \\(\\log_2 (\\log_2 n)\\)
Subtract \\(\\log_2 (\\log_2 n)\\) from both sides:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) - 4 = -1 \\]
Add 4 to both sides:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_2 n) = 3 \\]
Exponentiate both sides with base 2:
\\[ \\log_2 n = 2^3 = 8 \\]
Thus:
\\[ n = 2^8 = 256 \\]

#### Step 7: Verify the solution
Check that \\(n = 256\\) satisfies the original equation:
1. \\(\\log_{16} 256 = \\log_{16} 16^2 = 2\\) because \\(16^2 = 256\\).
2. \\(\\log_2 (\\log_{16} 256) = \\log_2 2 = 1\\).
3. \\(\\log_4 256 = \\log_4 4^3 = 3\\) because \\(4^3 = 64\\) is incorrect! Oops, this is a mistake. Actually, \\(4^3 = 64 \\neq 256\\), so this is incorrect.

Wait, this is a mistake. Let me re-calculate:
\\[ \\log_4 256 = \\frac{\\log_2 256}{\\log_2 4} = \\frac{8}{2} = 4 \\]
because \\(256 = 2^8\\) and \\(4 = 2^2\\).

But earlier, I thought \\(\\log_4 256 = 3\\) was incorrect. The correct value is 4.

Similarly, \\(\\log_4 n = \\log_4 256 = 4\\).

Thus, the right-hand side is:
\\[ \\log_4 (\\log_4 n) = \\log_4 4 = 1 \\]

But the left-hand side is:
\\[ \\log_2 (\\log_{16} n) = \\log_2 2 = 1 \\]

Thus, the original equation holds for \\(n = 256\\).

#### Step 8: Check the sum of digits
The sum of the digits of \\(256\\) in base 10 is:
\\[ 2 + 5 + 6 = 13 \\]

This matches the required condition.

#### Step 9: Uniqueness of the solution
We need to ensure that \\(n = 256\\) is the only solution. Suppose \\(n > 1\\) is a positive integer. The equation simplifies to \\(n = 256\\) as above.

### Step 10: Abstract Plan

1. **Simplify the given equation**:
   - Express all logarithms in terms of base 2.
   - Rewrite \\(\\log_{16} n\\) and \\(\\log_4 n\\) in terms of \\(\\log_2 n\\).

2. **Substitute and simplify**:
   - Substitute the simplified forms into the original equation.
   - Simplify both sides to find a relationship between \\(\\log_2 n\\).

3. **Solve for \\(n\\)**:
   - Derive \\(\\log_2 n = 8\\) from the simplified equation.
   - Conclude \\(n = 256\\).

4. **Verify the solution**:
   - Check that \\(n = 256\\) satisfies the original equation.
   - Compute the sum of digits of \\(256\\) to confirm it is \\(13\\).

### Lean 4 `have` Statements

```lean4
theorem amc12a_2020_p10
  (n : ℕ)
  (h₀ : 0 < n)
  (h₁ : Real.logb 2 (Real.logb 16 n) = Real.logb 4 (Real.logb 4 n)) :
  (Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
  have h_main : n = 256 := by
    sorry
  have h_sum : (Nat.digits 10 n).sum = 13 := by
    sorry
  exact h_sum
```

### Explanation
1. `h_main`: We first prove that `n = 256` is the only solution. This is the core of the proof, and it relies on the detailed simplification and solving steps above.
2. `h_sum`: Once we know `n = 256`, we can directly compute the sum of its digits in base 10 as `13`.

"""

proof = """
theorem mathd_numbertheory_447 :
  ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => 3∣x) (Finset.Icc 1 49), (k % 10) = 78 := by
  have h_main : ∑ k ∈ Finset.filter (fun x => 3∣x) (Finset.Icc 1 49), (k % 10) = 78 := by
    -- We will compute the sum by considering the remainders of each number divisible by 3 modulo 10.
    rfl
    <;> decide
    <;> simp [Finset.sum_filter, Finset.sum_range_succ, Nat.div_eq_of_lt]
    <;> norm_num
    <;> rfl
    <;> decide
  exact h_main
"""
outline = """
### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to find the sum of the remainders when each number in the set `{1, 2, ..., 49}` divisible by 3 is divided by 10. 

1. **Understand the Problem**:
   - We are summing the remainders when each number in `{1, 2, ..., 49}` divisible by 3 is divided by 10.
   - The numbers divisible by 3 in `{1, 2, ..., 49}` are `3, 6, 9, ..., 48`.
   - The remainders when these numbers are divided by 10 are:
     - `3 % 10 = 3`
     - `6 % 10 = 6`
     - `9 % 10 = 9`
     - `12 % 10 = 2`
     - `15 % 10 = 5`
     - `18 % 10 = 8`
     - `21 % 10 = 1`
     - `24 % 10 = 4`
     - `27 % 10 = 7`
     - `30 % 10 = 0`
     - `33 % 10 = 3`
     - `36 % 10 = 6`
     - `39 % 10 = 9`
     - `42 % 10 = 2`
     - `45 % 10 = 5`
     - `48 % 10 = 8`
   - The sum of these remainders is `3 + 6 + 9 + 2 + 5 + 8 + 1 + 4 + 7 + 0 + 3 + 6 + 9 + 2 + 5 + 8 = 78`.

2. **Formalize the Solution**:
   - The numbers divisible by 3 in `{1, ..., 49}` are `3 * k` for `k ∈ {1, ..., 16}` (since `3 * 16 = 48` and `3 * 17 = 51 > 49`).
   - The remainders modulo 10 are `3 * k % 10` for `k ∈ {1, ..., 16}`.
   - We can compute the sum of these remainders directly:
     - The remainders cycle every 10 numbers, but we can also observe that the remainders are periodic with a period of 10.
     - Alternatively, we can directly compute the sum by grouping the terms:
       - The remainders for `k ∈ {1, ..., 10}` are `3, 6, 9, 2, 5, 8, 1, 4, 7, 0`.
       - The remainders for `k ∈ {11, ..., 16}` are the same as for `k ∈ {1, ..., 6}`, but shifted by 10.
       - The sum is `(3 + 6 + 9 + 2 + 5 + 8 + 1 + 4 + 7 + 0) + (3 + 6 + 9 + 2 + 5 + 8) = 45 + 33 = 78`.
   - However, a more straightforward approach is to note that the remainders cycle every 10 numbers, and the sum of the first 10 remainders is `45`, and the second 6 remainders are the same as the first 6, but shifted by 10, so their sum is the same as the first 6. Thus, the total sum is `45 + 33 = 78`.

But to avoid confusion, we can also directly compute the sum:
   - The numbers divisible by 3 in `{1, ..., 49}` are `3, 6, 9, 12, 15, 18, 21, 24, 27, 30, 33, 36, 39, 42, 45, 48`.
   - The remainders modulo 10 are:

First, let's carefully restate the problem in a more familiar form. We have positive integers \\( x, y, n \\) (i.e., \\( x, y, n \\geq 1 \\)) and the equation:
\\[
\\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{n}
\\]
We need to prove that \\( n = 5 \\).

#### Step 1: Rewrite the Equation
Multiply both sides by \\( 12n \\) (the least common multiple of 4 and 6) to eliminate denominators:
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12(x + y)
\\]
This simplifies to:
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12x + 12y
\\]
Rearrange all terms to one side:
\\[
3n x + 2n y - 12x - 12y = 0
\\]
Factor out common terms:
\\[
(3n - 12)x + (2n - 12)y = 0
\\]

#### Step 2: Solve for \\( n \\)
Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the only way the equation can hold is if the coefficients of \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) are zero. However, this is not possible because \\( n \\geq 1 \\), and the coefficients are linear in \\( n \\). 

But wait, this is incorrect reasoning. The equation is:
\\[
(3n - 12)x + (2n - 12)y = 0
\\]
This is a linear Diophantine equation in \\( x \\) and \\( y \\). For \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the only solution is \\( x = y = 0 \\), which is impossible. 

But this is not correct either. The correct approach is to realize that the original equation is:
\\[
\\frac{x}{4} + \\frac{y}{6} = \\frac{x + y}{n}
\\]
Multiply through by \\( 12n \\):
\\[
3n x + 2n y = 12x + 12y
\\]
Rearrange:
\\[
(3n - 12)x + (2n - 12)y = 0
\\]
This can be rewritten as:
\\[
(3n - 12)x = - (2n - 12)y
\\]
Since \\( x, y \\geq 1 \\), the right-hand side is negative, and the left-hand side is positive (as \\( 3n - 12 > 0 \\) for \\( n \\geq 5 \\), and \\( x \\geq 1 \\)). 

But this is not directly helpful. A better approach is to consider the original equation as a relationship between \\( n \\), \\( x \\), and \\( y \\). 

#### Step 3: Find Possible \\( n \\)

    Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
    The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, recall that `σ : Equiv ℝ ℝ` is an equivalence (bijection) from `ℝ` to `ℝ`. This means that `σ` is a function with a two-sided inverse, and it is bijective. The condition `σ.1 2 = σ.2 2` is given, where `σ.1` and `σ.2` are the two components of the equivalence. However, Lean's `Equiv` is a structure with two projections: `toFun` and `invFun`, so `σ.1` is `σ.toFun` and `σ.2` is `σ.invFun`. 

But wait, this is incorrect! In Lean, `Equiv` is a structure with two projections: `toFun` and `invFun`, and the `2` in `σ.1 2` is a real number. The notation `σ.1` is `σ.toFun`, and `σ.2` is `σ.invFun`. 

But the condition `σ.1 2 = σ.2 2` is `σ.toFun 2 = σ.invFun 2`. 

The goal is to prove `σ.toFun (σ.toFun 2) = 2`.

#### Key Observations:
1. Since `σ` is an equivalence, `σ.toFun` is a bijection. In particular, it is injective.
2. The condition `σ.toFun 2 = σ.invFun 2` can be rewritten using the property of the inverse:
   - `σ.invFun 2 = σ.toFun⁻¹ 2` (but this is not standard notation).
   - Alternatively, `σ.invFun 2 = 2` because `σ.invFun` is the inverse of `σ.toFun`, and `σ.toFun 2 = 2` is not given.
   - But we can use the fact that `σ.toFun` is a bijection to deduce more.

But we can directly use the properties of `Equiv` to simplify the problem.

#### Step 1: Understand the Inverse Property
Since `σ` is an equivalence, `σ.toFun` is a bijection, and `σ.invFun` is its inverse. That is:
1. `σ.toFun (σ.invFun x) = x` for all `x ∈ ℝ`.
2. `σ.invFun (σ.toFun x) = x` for all `x ∈ ℝ`.

#### Step 2: Use the Given Condition
We are given `σ.toFun 2 = σ.invFun 2`.

    ```lean4
    theorem mathd_algebra_320
  (x : NNReal)
  (a b c : ℕ)
    ```

    The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, let's understand the problem:

We have a non-negative real number `x` (in Lean, `NNReal`). We have natural numbers `a`, `b`, `c` all greater than 0. We are given two equations:
1. `2 * x² = 4 * x + 9`
2. `x = (a + √b) / c`

We need to prove that `a + b + c = 26`.

#### Step 1: Solve the quadratic equation for `x`

The first equation is `2 * x² = 4 * x + 9`, which can be rewritten as:
`2 * x² - 4 * x - 9 = 0`.

This is a quadratic equation in `x`. We can solve it using the quadratic formula:
`x = (4 ± sqrt(16 + 72)) / 4 = (4 ± sqrt(88)) / 4 = (4 ± 2 * sqrt(22)) / 4 = (2 ± sqrt(22)) / 2`.

But wait, this is incorrect! The discriminant is `16 + 72 = 88`, not `88`. The correct discriminant is `16 + 72 = 88`, so `sqrt(88) = 2 * sqrt(22)`, and the roots are:
`x = (4 ± 2 * sqrt(22)) / 4 = (2 ± sqrt(22)) / 2`.

But `x` is a non-negative real number, and `sqrt(22) ≈ 4.69`, so `x = (2 + sqrt(22)) / 2 ≈ 3.345` and `x = (2 - sqrt(22)) / 2 ≈ -1.345` is invalid since `x ≥ 0`.

But Lean's `NNReal` is `≥ 0`, and `x` is a `NNReal`, so `x ≥ 0`. The only valid root is `x = (2 + sqrt(22)) / 2`.

But we can also consider `x = (2 - sqrt(22)) / 2`, but it is negative, which is invalid for `NNReal`.

But wait, the quadratic formula is `x = (-b ± sqrt(b² - 4ac)) / (2a)`, where `a = 2`, `b = -4`, `c = -9`.

The discriminant is `b² - 4ac = 16 - 4 * 2 * (-9) = 16 + 72 = 88`.

First, let's understand the problem:

We have a real number `a > 0` such that:
1. `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)`,
2. `2 < a²`,
3. `a² < 3`.

We need to prove that `a¹² - 144 * (1 / a) = 233`.

#### Observations:
1. From `2 < a² < 3`, we can deduce that `a` is in the interval `(√2, √3)`, because `a > 0` and `a² > 2` implies `a > √2`, and similarly `a² < 3` implies `a < √3`.
2. The condition `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)` is unusual. Let's denote `k = floor(1 / a)` and `m = floor(a²)`. Then the condition becomes:
   \\[
   \\frac{1}{a} - k = a² - m.
   \\]
   Rearranging gives:
   \\[
   \\frac{1}{a} - a² = k - m.
   \\]
   The right-hand side `k - m` is an integer, so `\\frac{1}{a} - a²` is an integer.
3. Since `2 < a² < 3`, we have `1/3 < 1/a² < 1/2` (because `a² > 2` implies `1/a² < 1/2` and `a² < 3` implies `1/a² > 1/3`).
   - This is because `a² > 2` implies `1/a² < 1/2` (since `a² > 2` and `1/a²` is decreasing in `a²`).
   - Similarly, `a² < 3` implies `1/a² > 1/3` (since `a² < 3` and `1/a²` is decreasing in `a²`).
   - But we can also directly compute `a²` from `2 < a² < 3` to get `a ≈ 1.732` (since `a² = 2.25` is too large, `a² = 2.24` is too small, etc.).
4. The condition `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)` is unusual because `a²` is in `(2, 3)`, so `floor(a²) = 2` (since `a² < 3` and `a² > 2`). Similarly, `1 / a` is in `(1/3, 1/2)` because `a² > 2` implies `1/a² < 1/2` and `a² < 3` implies `1/a² > 1/3`. But `a² > 2` implies `a < √3 ≈ 1.732`, so `1/a > 1/√3 ≈ 0.577`.    - But `floor(1/a)` is not immediately clear. For example, if `a² = 2.25` (`a = 1.5`), then `1/a = 2/3 ≈ 0.666`, so `floor(1/a) = 0`.
   - If `a² = 2.24` (`a ≈ 1.496`), `1/a ≈ 0.668`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.
   - If `a² = 2.23` (`a ≈ 1.493`), `1/a ≈ 0.669`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.

   - If `a² = 2.21` (`a ≈ 1.486`), `1/a ≈ 0.672`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.
   - If `a² = 2.205` (`a ≈ 1.484`), `1/a ≈ 0.673`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.
   - If `a² = 2.201` (`a ≈ 1.483`), `1/a ≈ 0.674`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.

   - If `a² = 2.20005` (`a ≈ 1.4832`), `1/a ≈ 0.6742`, `floor(1/a) = 0`.
   - It seems that `floor(1/a) = 0` in all these cases. But we need to confirm this.

But wait, let's think differently. The condition is `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)`. Since `a² ∈ (2, 3)`, `floor(a²) = 2`. So the condition becomes:
\\[
1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - 2.
\\]
But `a² - 2 ∈ (0, 1)`, so `1 / a - floor(1 / a) ∈ (0, 1)`.

\\[
1 / a = a² - 2.
\\]
But `a² - 2 ∈ (0, 1)`, so `1 / a ∈ (0, 1)`, i.e., `a > 1`.

But `a² < 3` and `a > 1` implies `a < √3 ≈ 1.732`.

But `a² - 2 = 1 / a` is equivalent to `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0`.

#### Approach:
1. From `2 < a² < 3`, we have `a ∈ (√2, √3)`.
2. The condition `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)` is unusual, but we can deduce that `floor(a²) = 2` because `a² ∈ (2, 3)`.
   - So `a² - floor(a²) = a² - 2 ∈ (0, 1)`.
   - The LHS is `1 / a - floor(1 / a)`, which is `1 / a - k` where `k` is an integer.
   - The RHS is `a² - 2 ∈ (0, 1)`, so `1 / a - k ∈ (0, 1)`.
   - But `a ∈ (√2, √3)`, so `1 / a ∈ (1/√3, 1/√2) ≈ (0.577, 0.707)`.
   - The only integer `k` satisfying `1 / a - k ∈ (0, 1)` is `k = 0` (since `1 / a - 1 ≤ 0` and `1 / a - 0 > 0`).
   - Thus, `floor(1 / a) = 0` and `1 / a - 0 = a² - 2`, i.e., `1 / a = a² - 2`.
3. The equation `1 / a = a² - 2` is `1 = a³ - 2a` or `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0`.
   - We can find `a` by solving `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0`.
   - The real root is `a ≈ 1.4142135623730950488016887242097`.
   - We can use the Intermediate Value Theorem to find that `a` is the unique real root in `(1, 2)`.
   - But we don't need the exact value.

   - Alternatively, we can use the fact that `a³ = 2a + 1` to find `a¹²` in terms of `a` and `a²`.
   - But this seems complicated.

#### Simplifying the condition:
From `1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - floor(a²)`, and `floor(a²) = 2` (since `a² ∈ (2, 3)`), we get:
\\[
1 / a - floor(1 / a) = a² - 2.
\\]
But `a² - 2 ∈ (0, 1)`, so `1 / a - floor(1 / a) ∈ (0, 1)`.

But `a ∈ (√2, √3)`, so `1 / a ∈ (1/√3, 1/√2) ≈ (0.577, 0.707)`.

Thus, `floor(1 / a) = 0` (since `1 / a > 0` and `1 / a < 1`).

Therefore, the condition simplifies to:
\\[
1 / a = a² - 2.
\\]
This is the same as `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0`.

#### Solving `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0`:
We can find the real root of `a³ - 2a - 1 = 0` in `(1, 2)`.

Let `f(a) = a³ - 2a - 1`.

`f(1) = 1 - 2 - 1 = -2 < 0`,
`f(2) = 8 - 4 - 1 = 3 > 0`.

We can find it numerically:
`a ≈ 1.4655712318767680266567312252656`.

But we don't need the exact value.

#### Calculating `a¹² - 144 * (1 / a)`:
From `1 / a = a² - 2`, we have `a³ = 2a + 1`.

We can compute `a¹²` in terms of `a` and `a²`:
`a¹² = (a³)⁴ = (2a + 1)⁴ = 16a⁴ + 32a³ + 24a² + 8a + 1`.

Thus:
`a¹² = 16(2a² + a) + 32(2a + 1) + 24a² + 8a + 1 = 32a² + 16a + 64a + 32 + 24a² + 8a + 1 = (32a² + 24a²) + (16a + 64a + 8a) + (32 + 1) = 56a² + 88a + 33`.

But this seems incorrect. Let's recompute:
`a¹² = (2a + 1)⁴ = (4a² + 4a + 1)² = 16a⁴ + 32a³ + 24a² + 8a + 1`.

But `a⁴ = a * a³ = a(2a + 1) = 2a² + a`.

Thus:
`a¹² = 16(2a² + a) + 32(2a + 1) + 24a² + 8a + 1 = 32a² + 16a + 64a + 32 + 24a² + 8a + 1 = (32a² + 24a²) + (16a + 64a + 8a) + (32 + 1) = 56a² + 88a + 33`.

`a¹² - 144 * (1 / a) = (56a² + 88a + 33) - (144a² - 288) = 56a² + 88a + 33 - 144a² + 288 = -88a² + 88a + 321`.

But this is not `233`. There seems to be a mistake.

Since `a² ∈ (2, 3)`, `floor(a²) = 2`.

\\[

#### Correct Calculation:
From `1 / a = a² - 2`, we have `a³ = 2a + 1`.

We can compute `a¹²` in terms of `a` and `a²`:
`a¹² = (a³)⁴ = (2a + 1)⁴ = 16a⁴ + 32a³ + 24a² + 8a + 1`.

But `a⁴ = a * a³ = a(2a + 1) = 2a² + a`.

`a¹² = 16(2a² + a) + 32(2a + 1) + 24a² + 8a + 1 = 32a² + 16a + 64a + 32 + 24a² + 8a + 1 = (32a² + 24a²) + (16a + 64a + 8a) + (32 + 1) = 56a² + 88a + 33`.

But this is not `233`. There seems to be a mistake.

`a¹² = (a³)⁴ = (2a + 1)⁴ = 16a⁴ + 32a³ + 24a² + 8a + 1`.

Thus:
`a¹² - 144 * (1 / a) = (56a² + 88a + 33) - (144a² - 288) = 56a² + 88a + 33 - 144a² + 288"""

   - Take absolute values: \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| = |a| \\).
   - But \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| \\leq |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\).
   - We can also use the fact that \\( m < n \\) and \\( m^3 = 2 \\), \\( n^3 = 4 \\) to bound \\( m \\) and \\( n \\).

9. **Final Contradiction:**
   - We can use the fact that \\( m^3 = 2 \\) and \\( n^3 = 4 \\) to bound \\( m \\) and \\( n \\).
   - Since \\( m > 0 \\), \\( m^3 = 2 \\), we have \\( m = 2^{1/3} \\approx 1.26 \\).
   - Similarly, \\( n = 4^{1/3} \\approx 1.59 \\).
   - The equation \\( a + b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = 0 \\) can be rewritten as \\( b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = -a \\).
   - The maximum possible value of \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| \\) is \\( |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\).
   - But \\( m < n \\), and \\( m^3 = 2 \\), \\( n^3 = 4 \\).
   - We can show that \\( |a| \\leq |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\), but this is not directly helpful.
   - Alternatively, we can use the fact that \\( m < n \\) and the given equations to derive a contradiction.

10. **Simpler Contradiction:**
    - Assume \\( a \\neq 0 \\). Then \\( |a| > 0 \\).
    - The equation \\( a + b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = 0 \\) implies \\( b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = -a \\).
    - Take absolute values: \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| = |a| \\).
    - But \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| \\leq |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\).

    - We can find that \\( m < n \\), and \\( m^3 = 2 \\), \\( n^3 = 4 \\).
    - We can also find that \\( m < 2^{1/3} \\approx 1.26 \\) and \\( n < 4^{1/3} \\approx 1.59 \\).
    - But we can also find that \\( m^2 = 2^{2/3} \\approx 1.587 \\), \\( n^2 = 4^{2/3} \\approx 2.52 \\).
    - This is getting complicated, so we can instead use the fact that \\( m^3 = 2 \\) and \\( n^3 = 4 \\) to find bounds.

    - This is getting complicated, so we can instead use the fact that \\( m^3 = 2 \\) and \\( n^3 = 4 \\) to find bounds.

14. **Conclusion:**
    - The only way the equation \\( a + b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = 0 \\) can hold is if \\( a = b = c = 0 \\).
    - This is because \\( m \\) and \\( n \\) are linearly independent in the sense that no non-trivial linear combination of them can be zero.
    - Alternatively, we can use the fact that \\( m^3 = 2 \\) and \\( n^3 = 4 \\) to find that \\( m \\) and \\( n \\) are linearly independent over the rationals.

#### Step-by-Step Abstract Plan:

1. **Assume \\( a \\neq 0 \\):**
   - The equation \\( a + b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = 0 \\) implies \\( b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n = -a \\).

2. **Take Absolute Values:**
   - \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| = |a| \\).

3. **Bound the Left Side:**
   - \\( |b \\cdot m + c \\cdot n| \\leq |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\).

4. **Use the Given Equations:**
   - \\( m^3 = 2 \\) and \\( n^3 = 4 \\).

5. **Find Contradiction:**
   - The maximum possible value of \\( |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\) is when \\( b \\) and \\( c \\) are as large as possible.
   - But \\( m < n \\), and \\( m^3 = 2 \\), \\( n^3 = 4 \\).
   - We can show that \\( |a| \\leq |b| \\cdot m + |c| \\cdot n \\) is impossible unless \\( a = b = c = 0 \\).

6. **Final Step:**
   - The only solution is \\( a = b = c = 0 \\).

<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜><｜User｜>Complete the following Lean 4 code:

    ```lean4

    theorem mathd_numbertheory_451
  (S : Finset ℕ)
  (h₀ : ∀ (n : ℕ), n ∈ S ↔ 2010 ≤ n ∧ n ≤ 2019 ∧ ∃ m, ((Nat.divisors m).card = 4 ∧ ∑ p ∈ (Nat.divisors m), p = n)) :
    ```

    Before producing the Lean 4 code to formally prove the given theorem, provide a detailed proof plan outlining the main proof steps and strategies.
    The plan should highlight key ideas, intermediate lemmas, and proof structures that will guide the construction of the final formal proof.<｜Assistant｜>### Detailed Proof and Analysis

First, we need to understand the problem:
- We have a set `S` of natural numbers where a natural number `n` is in `S` if and only if:
  1. `2010 ≤ n ≤ 2019`,
  2. There exists a natural number `m` such that:
     - The number of divisors of `m` is `4`, and
     - The sum of the divisors of `m` is `n`.
- We need to prove that the sum of all elements in `S` is `2016`.

#### Step 1: Identify the Elements of `S`

We need to find all numbers `n` in `[2010, 2019]` such that there exists a number `m` with exactly `4` divisors and the sum of its divisors is `n`.

First, recall that a number has exactly `4` divisors if and only if it is of one of the following forms:
1. `p³` where `p` is a prime.
2. `p * q` where `p` and `q` are distinct primes.

We will check each `n` in `[2010, 2019]` to see if it can be the sum of the divisors of some `m` with exactly `4` divisors.

#### Step 2: Check Each `n` in `[2010, 2019]`

We will check each `n` in `[2010, 2019]` to see if there exists a number `m` with exactly `4` divisors and sum of divisors `n`.

1. **`n = 2010`**:
   - Possible `m` with `4` divisors:
     - `p³` form: `m = p³` with `p` prime.
       - Sum of divisors: `1 + p + p² + p³ = 2010`.
       - `1 + p + p² + p³ = 2010` ⇒ `p³ + p² + p + 1 = 2010` ⇒ `p³ + p² + p = 2009`.
       - Try `p = 13`: `13³ = 2197`, `13² = 169`, `13 = 13`, sum is `2197 + 169 + 13 = 2379 > 2009`.
       - Try `p = 11`: `11³ = 1331`, `11² = 121`, `11 = 11`, sum is `1331 + 121 + 11 = 1463 > 2009`.
       - Try `p = 10`: `10³ = 1000`, `10² = 100`, `10 = 10`, sum is `1000 + 100 + 10 = 1110 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 12`: `12³ = 1728`, `12² = 144`, `12 = 12`, sum is `1728 + 144 + 12 = 1884 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 14`: `14³ = 2744`, `14² = 196`, `14 = 14`, sum is `2744 + 196 + 14 = 2954 > 2009`.
       - Try `p = 9`: `9³ = 729`, `9² = 81`, `9 = 9`, sum is `729 + 81 + 9 = 819 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 8`: `8³ = 512`, `8² = 64`, `8 = 8`, sum is `512 + 64 + 8 = 584 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 7`: `7³ = 343`, `7² = 49`, `7 = 7`, sum is `343 + 49 + 7 = 399 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 6`: `6³ = 216`, `6² = 36`, `6 = 6`, sum is `216 + 36 + 6 = 258 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 5`: `5³ = 125`, `5² = 25`, `5 = 5`, sum is `125 + 25 + 5 = 155 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 4`: `4³ = 64`, `4² = 16`, `4 = 4`, sum is `64 + 16 + 4 = 84 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 3`: `3³ = 27`, `3² = 9`, `3 = 3`, sum is `27 + 9 + 3 = 39 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 2`: `2³ = 8`, `2² = 4`, `2 = 2`, sum is `8 + 4 + 2 = 14 < 2009`.
       - Try `p = 1`: `1³ = 1`, `1² = 1`, `1 = 1`, sum is `1 + 1 + 1 = 3 < 2009`.
     - `p * q` form: `m = p * q` with `p` and `q` distinct primes.
       - Sum of divisors: `1 + p + q + p * q = 2010`.
       - We need `1 + p + q + p * q = 2010`.

   - Possible `m` with `4` divisors:
     - `p³` form: `m = p³` with `p` prime.
